line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">The function of taking cognizance by a Judicial Magistrate is an important step in our criminal justice system and, in taking cognizance, while, in a CR case, a Judicial Magistrate is duty bound to examine and consider the complaint petition, statements, made by the complainant under Section 200 </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">CrPC and if there is a Judicial Enquiry, then,</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> the Judicial Enquiry Report and, likewise,<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>in the GR case, the FIR, the statements made by the witnesses under Sections 161 and 164 CrPC, the statements made by the accused under Section 164 CrPC, the police </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">report and the other incriminating materials,</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> but s/he is not bound by the findings and recommendations of the police report or the <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">Judicial Enquiry Report, for, the law </span></span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">envisages</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.5pt;"> that the cognizance-taking Magistrate</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> shall be </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">completely independent in having the primary</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> satisfaction as to whether there is any <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">prima facie</em> evidence of commission of offence by any accused for taking cognizance. . . .(13)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure (V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Sections 190(1)(b) and 190(1)(c)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 105%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.5pt;">When a criminal case is filed in the Police Station</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%;"> by lodging an FIR and, after investigation of the said case, the Investigating Officer (IO) submits a police report recommending for </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">prosecuting or discharging any accused person,</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%;"> the Magistrate is not bound by the recom-mendation made by the IO for discharging any accused; rather the Magistrate is at liberty to take cognizance of any offence against any person either on the basis of the materials available before the Magistrate, such as, the police report or on the basis of his own knowledge/suspicion or information received from any person other than a police officer under Section 190 (1)(b) or under Section 190 (1)(c) of the CrPC.<span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">                              </span>. . .(14)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure (V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Sections 190(1)(a) and 200</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">When a criminal case is filed through Court by presenting a petition of complaint/oral </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">complaint before a Magistrate, the Magistrate</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> is at liberty either to take cognizance directly under Section 190 (1)(a) of the CrPC on the basis of the statements made by the complainant under Section 200 CrPC or, in the event that the complaint is sent for Judicial Enquiry, on the basis of Judicial Enquiry Report.<span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">     </span>. . . (14)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure (V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Section 190</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">In a GR case, after submission of the police report by the IO when a Naraji petition (complaint petition) is filed and the Magistrate directs for Judicial Enquiry, then, upon receiving the Judicial Enquiry Report, the Magistrate is at liberty to take cognizance either on the basis of the Judicial Enquiry Report, treating the same as <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">‘information received from any person other than a police officer’</em> under Section 190 (1)(c) of the CrPC or on the basis of his own knowledge/suspicion under Section 190 of the CrPC or on the basis of the police report under Section 190 (1)(b) of the CrPC or on </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">the basis of complaint (Naraji petition) under</span></strong> <strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">Section 190 (1)(a) of the CrPC following finding</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">prima-facie</em> truthfulness as to the allegation made in the complaint petition through carrying out Judicial Enquiry.<span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">       </span>. . . (15)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure (V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Section 190</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Only source of the power of taking cognizance of any offence by the Magistrates of Bangladesh is Section 190 of the CrPC and, therefore, at the time of taking cognizance, it is a pivotal duty for a Magistrate to see whether taking cognizance of any offence fits into or relates to any of the clauses of sub-Section (1) of Section 190 of the CrPC. Thus, a Judicial Magistrate or </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.5pt;">an Executive Magistrate having been empowered</span></strong> <strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.1pt;">to take cognizance by the Government under</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> Section 190(4) of the CrPC is competent to take cognizance of any offence against any person in addition to the person recomme-nded by the IO and/or the Enquiring Magistrate resorting to any of the provisions which are enumerated under sub-Section (1) of Section 190 of the CrPC.<span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">           </span>. . . (15)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure (V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Sections 190 and 203</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">After receiving the Judicial Enquiry Report, when the Magistrate is going to pass an order on taking cognizance of any offence against any person/s under Section 190 (1) of the CrPC or dismissal of the complaint under Section 203 of the CrPC, it is incumbent upon the Magistrate to examine and consider the nitty-gritty of the Judicial Enquiry Report. In other words, to meticulously look at the statements of JWs and the comments on the demeanour of the JWs, findings and observations on other steps taken by the Enquiring Magistrate and, then, upon considering the above materials, if the Magistrate is <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">prime-facie</em> satisfied that the report prepared by the Enquiring Magistrate, recommending for prosecuting all the accused named in the complaint petition or opining to prosecute a few and discharge others or proposing for discharge of all the accused, is entirely acceptable, s/he may either accept the same in toto or may take cognizance against the not-sent-up accused together with the sent-up accused upon recording her/his own opinion on the Judicial Enquiry Report so as to be evident on record that the Magistrate </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.5pt;">did not seek to stereotypically agree or disagree</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> with the comments/ remarks/ findings/ observations made or the views expressed by the Enquiring Magistrate and, thereby, the </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">cognizance-taking Magistrate has independently</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> formed her/his own opinion in taking cognizance of the offence or dismissing the petition of complaint.<span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">        </span>. . .(17)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure (V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Section 190</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">The power of taking cognizance of an offence against any accused is vested in the Magistrates by the Legislature vide Section 190 of the CrPC and, therefore, it is the Magistrate who should be satisfied as to whether cognizance is to be taken or not against any person, be s/he is recommended by the IO/Enquiring Magistrate or not and, thus, the function of taking cognizance being the exclusive business of the Magistrates, passing an order of direction upon the Magistrate to take cognizance is beyond the competence of the Sessions Judge and High Court Division in exercising their appellate or revisional jurisdiction. . . . (26)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure (V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Section 561A</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 105%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%;">By exercising the power under Section 561A of the CrPC, in a fit and proper case, which may be found in the rarest of rare cases, the High Court Division is well competent to direct the Magistrate to take cognizance towards securing the ends </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">of justice. For example, from the prosecution</span></strong> <strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.5pt;">materials when any one with ordinary prudence</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%;"> would be able to sum up that 1+1 becomes 2, making the above result to be 1.50 or 2.50 by a Magistrate would be a perverse decision and, in that scenario, interference by the High Court Division exercising its inherent power would be quite justified.<span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">                                            </span>. . . (26)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure (V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Sections 190, 200, 202, 203, 204, 435, 436, 439, 439A and 561 A</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Upon receiving the Naraji petition against the police report if a Judicial Enquiry is conducted and on consideration of the police report, Judicial Enquiry Report and other prosecution materials, the Magistrate does not take cognizance of an offence against </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">any accused by dismissing the Naraji petition</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> (complaint) wholly/partly under Section 203 of the CrPC and the informant-complainant seeks to revise the order directly from the High Court Division without preferring a revision before the Court of Sessions Judge, the High Court Division’s proper order would be to direct further enquiry. But when the informant-complainant takes recourse to the inherent power under Section 561A of the CrPC, irrespective of the fact whether the petitioner invoked the inherent power directly or after exhausting the revisional forum of the Court of Sessions Judge, on the ground that there is no need for further enquiry in the backdrop of availability of the evidence already obtained through Judicial Enquiry, the High Court Division is not powerless to direct the CJM </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">or the concerned Magistrate to take cognizance</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> of an offence against any accused against whom <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">prima facie</em> evidence is very much evident in the Judicial Enquiry Report subject to the condition that the High Court Division is satisfied that the order of the </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">Magistrate in dismissing the Naraji (complaint)</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> entirely/partly was perverse or ex-facie wrong being manifestly at variance with the evidence which the Enquiring Magistrate obtained. The rationale behind holding the above view is that when the informant, upon disagreeing with the outcome of the investigation by the police, opts for Judicial Enquiry and the Judicial Enquiry reveals commission of an offence against the accused named in the complaint petition, but the Magistrate does not take </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">cognizance, there is no need to conduct further</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> enquiry as the informant-complainant is not raising grievance against the Judicial Enquiry; the complainant’s grievance in the said situation is only against the Order of the cognizance-taking Magistrate. And when the CJM/the concerned Magistrate would be </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">asked to consider the Judicial Enquiry Report</span></strong> <strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">afresh and, pursuant thereto, if the Magistrate</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> passes the self-same order, sending back the </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">matter to the CJM/concerned Magistrate would</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> turn to be an exercise in futility.<span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">  </span>. . . (27)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Code of Criminal Procedure (V of 1898)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Sections 190(1) and 203</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Ends of justice would be met if we direct the CJM to hear the issue of taking cognizance upon judiciously evaluating the evidence available in the file, in particular, the statements of the JWs and, thereby, pass an appropriate order of taking cognizance of the alleged offences against all the accused or a few, either under Section 190(1) of the </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">CrPC or dismiss the Naraji petition (complaint)</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> wholly/party under Section 203 of the CrPC. . . . (28)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Constitutions of Bangladesh, 1972</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Article 109</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 6pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">It is the Constitutional duty of the Court under Article 109 of the Constitution to set down some guidelines for the learned Sessions Judges who deal with the criminal revision applications against the Orders and, also, for the learned Magistrates assigned as Enquiring Magistrates and cognizance-taking Magistrates with an expectation that they would endeavour to be accurate in passing Judicial Orders and help the Judiciary to save invaluable working hours, which are being spent in dealing with Revisional and Miscellaneous matters and, thereby, establish and uphold the majesty and magnanimity of the Judiciary. </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.25in; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><u><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.6pt;">Guidelines for the learned Sessions Judges:</span></u></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(i)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">     </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">In exercising the criminal revisional power bestowed upon the learned Sessions Judges, they should not </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">shrug off their duty and responsibility</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> of revising the impugned Judgment/ Order by simply agreeing or disagreeing with the lower Court’s Judgment/Order.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(ii)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">   </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">They are statutorily obligated to delve deep into the question of law in the context of the given facts of the concerned case and, then, record their own view/s reflecting their independent performance and competence. </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(iii) </span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">They must be judicious and quick in disposing of the revisional matters filed against any Order so that the general people cannot blame the judiciary that the enquiry or trial of a case is delayed due to the poor and slow performance of the Courts. </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(iv)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">  </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Immediately before retirement from </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">service, while many of the Government</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> servants of this country possess a tendency to be in dereliction of their respective duties and work in a cavalier fashion, the learned District and Sessions Judges must not hold the aforesaid mentality; rather they </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">should be more serious in performing</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> their duties at such juncture of their </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">service, so that their junior colleagues,</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> the Supreme Court, the Advocates and the Court-staff remember them forever with admiration.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(v)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">    </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">They should put in their best efforts </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">to earn recognition from the citizenry</span></strong> <strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">that the Judiciary is the most dynamic</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> and patriotic organ of the State, being manned and run by honest, brilliant, vigilant and skilled officers.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(vi)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">  </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">In collaboration with the CMM/ CJM, they shall hold, at least once in a month, a Judicial conference with all the Judges and Magistrates at the office of the District/Metropolitan Sessions Judge with a view to knowing the hurdles/problems they are facing in carrying out their performance and, then, seek to find solution in the light of the provisions of (1) Civil Rules and Orders and (2) Criminal Rules and Orders. There should be a special session in the conference, where the Sessions Judges and CMM/CJM shall share the interpretations on the provisions of procedural laws of the land (namely, CPC &amp; CrPC) settled by the Apex Court.<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.25in; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><u><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Guidelines for the learned Magistrates who would be assigned for conducting Judicial Enquiry: </span></u></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">i)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">        </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">While the primary duty of an Enquiring Magistrate is to take statements from the witnesses whose names are cited in the petition of </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">complaint/Naraji petition, s/he should</span></strong> <strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">also take statements from the persons</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> who appear to the Enquiring Magistrate to be relevant witnesses.<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 107%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 107%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">ii)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">      </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 107%;">An Enquiring Magistrate should endeavour to visit and inspect the place of occurrence, if it transpires to her/him to be useful for the purpose of ascertaining the veracity of the allegations made in the complaint/Naraji petition.<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">iii)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">    </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">In preparation of the Judicial Enquiry</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> Report, an Enquiring Magistrate should record the demeanour of the </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">JWs, if there was something noticeable,</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> in addition to stating her/his own findings and opinion on the works done by her/him. </span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">iv)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">    </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">An Enquiring officer must try to complete the judicial enquiry within the shortest possible time.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.25in; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><u><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Guidelines for the learned Magistrates who take cognizance:</span></u></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">i)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">        </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span>In taking cognizance, a Magistrate must minutely examine all the available prosecution materials,<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>namely, FIR, sketch map, Index, seizure list, medical certificate, post-mortem report and Inquest report (in case of homicidal offence), the statements made by the witnesses under Sections 161 and 164 of the CrPC, the statements/confessions made by the accused under Section 164 of the CrPC, case diary, for, the police report under Section 190 (1)(b) of the CrPC does not mean only the report prepared by the IO, but it also includes all the materials mentioned above. And if there is any Judicial Enquiry, the cognizance-taking Magistrate must examine all the materials of the Judicial Enquiry Report, which include the statements of the JWS, together with the remarks on the demeanour of the JWs, findings and observations on the seized goods/articles seen by the </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">own eyes of the Enquiring Magistrate,</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> on the crime spot/other places visited and inspected under Section 539B </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.5pt;">CrPC and other incriminating materials.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">ii)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">      </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">In a CR Case, be it based on oral/ written petition or Naraji petition, when a Magistrate takes statement under Section 200 CrPC, s/he should precisely record the statements of the complainant from which any one may be able to understand the nature of the allegation.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">iii)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">    </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">A Magistrate should be satisfied from the statements made under Section 200 CrPC that there is prime facie ingredient to take cognizance of any offence.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">iv)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">    </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">If it is a complaint under Section 138 of the NI Act, a Magistrate must follow the guidelines laid down in the case of Aleya Vs the State 12 ALR 2018(1) HCD 90.</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 0.5in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 105%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">v)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">      </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">A Magistrate must get satisfaction on</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%;"> his/her own before taking cognizance of an offence against any person. S/he must not confine her/himself to the proposal/recommendation made </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">by the I/O or the Enquiring Magistrate.</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%;"> While s/he is free to take cognizance </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">against any accused not recommended/</span></strong> <strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.5pt;">proposed by the I/O or the Enquiring</span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%;"> Magistrate, s/he is not competent to drop off any person against whom charge-sheet has been filed and/or recommendations have been made by the Enquiring Magistrate. . . .(29)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Anwaruddin Molla Vs. Hamid Molla, 18 DLR 295; Md. Akbar Hossain Vs Hasanul Hoque Inu, 11 BLT (AD) 166; Bangladesh Vs Yakub Sardar 40 DLR (AD) 246; Yousuf A Hossain </span></em></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.2pt;">Vs KM Rezaul Firdous 48 DLR (AD) 53 and </span></em></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.5pt;">Aleya Vs The State, 12 ALR 2018(1) HCD 90 ref.</span></em></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-left: 0.5in; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Mr. Sarwar Ahmed, Advocate</span></p><p align="right" class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 0.5in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">. . .For the petitioner</span></em></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-left: 0.5in; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Mr.</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;"> Md.</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;"> Sultan Mahmud, Advocate </span></p><p align="right" class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 0.5in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">. . . For the opposite party No. 2</span></em></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-left: 0.5in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Mr.</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> Md.</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> Khurshedul Alam, D.A.G</span></p><p align="right" class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 0.5in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">. . . For the State</span></em></strong></p><p align="center" class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><u><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">JUDGMENT</span></u></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; text-indent: 24pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><u><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">Muhammad Khurshid Alam Sarkar, J:</span></u></strong> <span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; letter-spacing: -.4pt;">At</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> the instance of the above named informant-Naraji applicant-petitioner (hereinafter referred to either as the informant or the complainant or the petitioner), this application has been filed by invoking this Court’s power of quashment under Section 561A of the Code of Criminal <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">Procedure, 1898 (CrPC) in an expectation to set</span> aside the Judgment and Order dated 09.01.2014 passed by the learned Senior Sessions Judge, Gaibandha in Criminal Revision No. 271 of 2012 and, also, the Judgment and Order dated 24.07.2012 passed by the learned Senior Judicial Magistrate, Gaibandha in G.R. Case No. 205 of 2009 (Gobindagonj) arising out of Gobindagonj Police Station Case No. 22 dated 11.05.2009 (presently Sessions Case No. 372 of 2012) taking cognizance against the charge-sheeted <span style="letter-spacing: -.4pt;">accused persons under Sections 302/201/34 of the</span> <span style="letter-spacing: -.4pt;">Penal Code and discharging the four FIR-named</span> accused persons after holding Judicial Enquiry.<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">2.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">The background of issuance of this Rule, in short, is that one Md. Aynul Hoque alias Abdul Mannan, as the informant, lodged an FIR alleging, <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">inter-alia</em>, that on 10.05.2009 at about 08.30 hours his son Ahsan Habib Pinu (hereinafter referred to as the victim) went to the local Bazar and when the victim was not returning home, the informant and other relatives rushed to every possible places and, eventually, a 12-year old minor boy, who is a distant grandson of the informant, found the head of the victim floating in the latrine of one Md. Liaqat Ali and therefrom they pulled out the dead body of the victim. The informant alleged in the FIR that there was a long-standing dispute over establishment of an educational institution between the informant and Liakat Ali and, for which, the informant suspects that (1) Md. Liakat Ali along with (2) Md Bablu Mia, (3) Md. Manik Mia, (4) Md Ruhul Amin and (5) Md Rakibul Islam killed his son and hid the dead body in the latrine. Upon receiving the above FIR, the police investigated the matter and submitted charge-sheet on 29.03.2010 under Sections 302/201/34 of the Penal Code against Md. Babul Mia and Harunur Rashid and, at the same time, final report was presented in favour of four FIR-named accused, including Md. Liakat Ali, which prompted the informant to file a Naraji Petition. On considering the averments of the said Naraji Petition, the Court on 02.08.2010 ordered for conducting a Judicial Enquiry by a Judicial Magistrate. Accordingly, a Judicial Magistrate from the judicial magistracy at the District of Gaibandha (hereinafter referred to as the Enquiring Magistrate), after conducting the Judicial Enquiry, on 30.11.2011 came up with the same findings as were recorded by the police and, then, the learned Senior Judicial Magistrate, Gaibandha (hereinafter referred to as the cognizance-taking Magistrate) took cognizance against 2 (two) charge-sheeted accused persons and discharged the other not-sent-up four accused persons vide his order dated 24.07.2012. Being aggrieved by and dissatisfied with the same, the informant filed a revisional application before the Senior Sessions Judge, Gaibandha who dismissed the revisional application upholding the Order passed by the Senior Judicial Magistrate, <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">Gaibandha. Challenging the legality and propriety</span> <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">of the above two Orders, the informant-petitioner</span> approached this Court under Section 561A of the CrPC and hence the Rule.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">3.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">Mr. Sarwar Ahmed, the learned Advocate appearing on behalf of the informant-petitioner, takes us through the FIR and, side by side, the statements made by the Judicial Witnesses (JWs) and submits that while the FIR merely contains the suspicion by the informant against Md. Liakat Ali, Md. Babul Miah, Md. Manik Miah, Md. Ruhul Amin and Raqibul Islam, the statements of JWs clearly suggest that they were involved in the murder. He, then, draws our attention to the report prepared by the Enquiring Magistrate and submits that the Enquiring Magistrate, having not judiciously evaluated the statements of the JWs and having<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>come to the conclusion that nothing was found against Md. Liakat Ali and his<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>3 cohorts, committed a serious illegality. He next submits that the cognizance-taking Magistrate utterly failed to perform his duty in <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">having his own satisfaction and to independently</span> decide as to whether cognizance should be taken against the non-proposed accused. In support of his above count of submissions, he refers to the cases of Anwaruddin Molla Vs Hamid Molla 18 DLR 295 and Md. Akbar Hossain Vs Hasanul Hoque Inu 11 BLT (AD) 166. Mr. Sarwar Ahmed, thereafter, takes us through the Judgment passed by the learned Sessions Judge in disposing of the revisional application and submits that the revisional application was disposed of in a perfunctory manner without applying his judicial mind and comments that it is simply unbefitting for a Senior Sessions Judge to write such a Judgment, given that the same is nothing but a mere replication of the Order passed by the learned Magistrate. Mr. Sarwar Ahmed submits that since, in this case, anyone with ordinary prudence upon considering the JWs’ statements would be of the view that there is <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">a prima facie</em> case against the FIR-named suspected persons, this Court, instead of remanding the case to the cognizance-taking Magistrate for further enquiry, should direct the Magistrate to take cognizance against all the suspected persons straightaway. In response to a query made by this Court as to whether this Court is competent to direct the Magistrate to take cognizance, he professes that since the present application is not under Section 436 of the CrPC but under Section 561A of the CrPC, this is a unique case where this Court is amply empowered to straightaway direct the Magistrate concerned to take cognizance of the offence against the not-sent-up accused. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">4.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">By making the above submissions, the learned Advocate for the petitioner prays for setting aside the Judgment and Order dated 09.01.2014 passed by the learned Senior Sessions Judge, Gaibandha in Criminal Revision No. 271 of 2012 and, also, the Judgment and Order dated 24.07.2012 passed by the learned Senior Judicial Magistrate, Gaibandha in G.R. Case No. 205 of 2009 (Gobindagonj) arising out of Gobindagonj Police Station Case No. 22 dated 11.05.2009 which presently is pending before the Court of learned Sessions Judge bearing Sessions Case No. 372 of 2012.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">5.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Per contra, Mr. Md. Sultan Mahmud, the learned Advocate appearing on behalf of the opposite party no. 2, takes us through the Final Report as well as the Judicial Enquiry Report and submits that since the investigation carried out by the police department tallies with the outcome of the Judicial Enquiry, this Court should not interfere with the Orders passed by the learned cognizance-taking Magistrate and the learned Senior Sessions Judges. He, then, submits that while in a fit and proper case, this Court sometimes orders for further enquiry, but this is not a case of that kind warranting further enquiry by the Magistrate or police. He submits that it is the settled principle of our jurisdiction that the High Court Division is not competent to pass any direction upon the Magistrate to take cognizance of the offences alleged. In support of his submissions, he refers to the cases of Bangladesh Vs Yakub Sardar 40 DLR (AD) 246 and Yousuf A Hossain Vs KM Rezaul Firdous 48 DLR (AD) 53. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">6.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">After hearing the submissions of both the sides, having perused the application together with its annexures (FIR, charge-sheet, Naraji application, statements of the JWs, report of the Enquiring Magistrate, Order of taking cognizance by the Magistrate and the Order passed by the Senior Sessions Judge in criminal revision) and upon reading the relevant laws and decisions, it appears to us that this Court is required to adjudicate upon the following legal issues, namely, (i) whether the Enquiring Magistrate carried out the Judicial Enquiry properly and, then, prepared a Judicial Enquiry report appropriately, (ii) whether the Magistrate dealt with the issue of taking cognizance accurately and (iii) whether this Court is empowered to direct the Magistrate to take cognizance, if the impugned Orders are quashed/set aside by us.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">7.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">Let us take up the first issue, namely, whether the Enquiring Magistrate carried out <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">the Judicial Enquiry properly and, then, prepared</span> a Judicial Enquiry report appropriately. It would be profitable for adjudication upon this issue, if we try to be acquainted with the terms ‘Enquiry’ and ‘Judicial Enquiry’, at first. While the definition of the term ‘enquiry’ is available, having been provided in Section 4(1)(k) of the CrPC in the following wordings <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">“every enquiry other than a trial conducted under this Code by a Magistrate or Court”</em>, there is no such <span style="letter-spacing: -.4pt;">statutory definition of the phraseology “Judicial</span> Enquiry”. The Black’s Law Dictionary inscribes the meaning of the phraseology “Judicial Enquiry” in the following wordings <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">“an official in-Court investigation of events, facts and actions to address a question of law and render an opinion”.</em> Commonly, Judicial Enquiry is known to be an enquiry, of any accident or crime, carried out by a Judge or Judicial Magistrate upon being directed by a Court of law/by the President of the Country/by the Parliament/by the Cabinet/by the Prime Minister. While for carrying out investigation by the police officers, there are prescribed provisions in the CrPC and the Police Regulations of Bengal, 1943, there is no such statutory procedural provisions or Apex Court’s guidelines in place for conducting a Judicial Enquiry. From the perspective of criminal proceedings, while the statutory source of commencing a Judicial Enquiry is Section 202 of the CrPC, Section 539B of the CrPC provides the extent of activities of a Judicial Enquiry. Therefore, Judicial Enquiry in connection with criminal proceedings means an enquiry of complaint of any offence ordered by a Judicial Magistrate to be carried out by the same Judicial Magistrate or by another Judicial Magistrate for the purpose of ascertaining the truth or falsehood of the complaint and, in ascertaining the truth or falsehood of the complaint, the Enquiring Magistrate may do anything which s/he considers to be relevant and appropriate, including taking evidence of witnesses on oath under Section 202(2A) CrPC and visiting/inspecting the crime spot and other place/s, as mandated by Section 539B of the CrPC. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">8.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">The Enquiring Magistrate, at the end of carrying out a Judicial Enquiry, is required to prepare a Judicial Enquiry Report on the basis of the statements made by the JWs together with their remarks/comments about the demeanour of the JWs, if any demeanour is ever noticed by the Enquiring Magistrate, as well as the findings and observations on other step/s taken by the Enquiring Magistrate, such as visiting the place of occurrence under Section 539B of the CrPC, taking snaps of the place of occurrence and the injury on the body, etc. In course of preparation of a Judicial Enquiry Report, the Enquiring Magistrate should seek to form her/his own opinion without being influenced by the findings and proposals made by the IO in the report submitted under Section 173 of the CrPC and the same should be forwarded to the Chief Metropolitan/Judicial Magistrate who, then her/himself, or any other Magistrate assigned by the CMM/CJM, would independently consider the issue of taking cognizance under Section 190 CrPC or dismissal of the complaint under Section 203 CrPC upon examining all the materials of the police report, namely, FIR, sketch map, index, seizure list, medical certificate, post-mortem report &amp; inquest report (in case of any homicidal offence), the statements made by the witnesses under Sections 161 &amp; 164 of the CrPC, the statements/confessions made by the accused under Section 164 of the CrPC, case diary, for, the police report under Section 190 (1)(b) of the CrPC does not mean only the report prepared by the IO, but it also includes all the materials mentioned above, and, also, upon examining all the materials of the Judicial Enquiry Report, which includes the statements of the JWS, together with the remarks on the demeanour of the JWs, findings and observations on the seized goods/articles seen by the own eyes of the Enquiring Magistrate, on the crime spot/other places visited and inspected under Section 539B CrPC and other incriminating materials. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">9.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">Now, let us look at the facts of this case. From a minute perusal of the annexed papers, it transpires that the FIR was lodged on 11.05.2009 under Sections 302/201/34 of the Penal Code against 5 (five) suspected persons namely, (i) Md. Liakat Ali (ii) Md. Bablu Mia (iii) Md. Manik Mia (iv) Md. Ruhul Amin and (v) Md. Rakibul Islam and, after the investigation, the Investigating Officer (IO) on 29.03.2010 while proposing to prosecute Md. Babul Mia and another person, named, Md. Harun Ur Rashid who was not named in the FIR, recommended to release other 4 suspected persons. When the informant filed a Naraji application, the learned Magistrate vide his Order dated 02.08.2010 ordered a Judicial Enquiry. Upon skimming through the materials of the Judicial Enquiry, it appears that the learned Enquiring Magistrate did not visit and inspect the place of occurrence or did not make any effort to take evidence from any witnesses other than those witnesses whose names were furnished by the complainant. Names of four witnesses were furnished by the complainant and their statements were taken on oath by the Enquiring Magistrate. JW1 is the informant <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">himself, JW2 is the brother of the informant and</span> <span style="letter-spacing: -.4pt;">JW3 &amp; JW4 appear to us to be neutral witnesses.</span> All these 4 (four) JWs made their statements <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">between 02.09.2010 to 19.09.2010. The statements</span> of two neutral witnesses, namely, the JW3 &amp; JW4 are quoted below respectively: </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 27.5pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">“</span><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: SutonnyBanglaMJ;">MZ 11/5/2009 Bs ZvwiL mKvj Abygvb 8/9 Uvi w`‡K †g_i wcbyi jvk DVvB‡ZwQj| wjqvK‡Zi †jwU«b Gi wfZi n‡Z| Avwg †mmgq Dcw¯’Z wQjvg| Zvi Av‡Mi w`b 10/5/2009 Bs ZvwiL Abygvb 20/10-30 Uvi w`‡K wjqvKZ, i“ûj, iwdKzj, gvwbK I eveyj wg‡j wcby‡K †U‡b wb‡q hvB‡Z †`wL| wcby‡K Mjv wU‡c a‡i wb‡q hvB‡ZwQj| wcbyi wcZv‡K Avwg G K_v ewjwb| Avgvi k¦ïi evox KjvKvUv nvg¯’vcyi n‡Z Avmvi c‡_ Avwg Zv †`‡LwQjvg| Z‡e Avwg wcby‡K nZ¨v Kiv †`wLwb| GB Avgvi Revbew›`|</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">”</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 27.5pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">“</span><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: SutonnyBanglaMJ;">MZ 11/5/2009 Bs ZvwiL mKvj 8/8.30 Uvi w`‡K wcbyi jvk DVv‡bv †`wL| jvk DVv‡bv nq wjqvKZ Gi evoxi cvqLvbvi wfZi n‡Z| cywjk I A‡bK †jvK wQj| 2 Rb †g_i DVvBqv wQj| Zvi Av‡Mi w`b ivZ 8/8.30 Uvi w`‡K eyoveywo Rvsjv evRvi n‡Z evRvi K‡i evox hvIqvi c‡_ eyoveywo nvB¯‹yj gv‡Vi cwð‡g wcbymn i“ûj Avwgb, (†jLvcov K‡i) iwdKzj Bmjvg (†jLvcov K‡i) eveyj (K¨v‡mU †gKvi) gvwbK (msmvix K‡i) I wjqvKZ gv÷vi‡K †`wL| Gi †ekx wKQy †`wLwb ev Rvwbbv| †mw`b e„wó nw”Qj ¸wo¸wo K‡i| GB Avgvi Revbew›`|</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">”</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">10.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Even if it is presumed that the learned Enquiring Magistrate did not consider it handy to visit the crime-spot or any other place and also did not feel beneficial to take evidence from any person other than the witnesses cited in the petition of complaint, let us vet whether the findings and observations made by the Enquiring Magistrate reflect the statements made by the JWs. The learned Enquiring Magistrate’s report dated 30.10.2011 contains the following findings and observations: </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 27.5pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">“In view of the Order dated 02.08.2010, this Court issued notice upon JWs and after examining them it appears to this Court that the two sent-up accused mentioned in the police report have overt acts and there is no direct and substantive <u>intelligence</u> against the not-sent-up accused. For taking cognizance or any necessary order, this report along with the statements of JWs is sent accordingly” (the underlined word “intelligence” is, perhaps, meant to be involvement/participation/abetment).<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">11.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">From a bare reading of the above statements made by the JW3 &amp; JW4, it is evident that the victim was last seen with the FIR-named suspected persons near the place of occurrence, but it appears to us that the Enquiring Magistrate utterly failed to notice the said crucial event and did not record the said important aspect in his findings. It further appears to us from the above report submitted by the Enquiring Magistrate that he tagged up and relied on the findings of the police in preparing the Judicial Enquiry Report. Therefore, we hold that the Judicial Enquiry was not carried out properly, and the Judicial Enquiry Report was not prepared appropriately. However, our above findings are not capable of vitiating the Judicial Enquiry, for, inspection of the place of occurrence was a discretionary step for the Enquiring<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>Magistrate and, also, the failure to prepare an appropriate Judicial Enquiry Report is a mere irregularity, which always can be cured at the stage of consideration of the same by the cognizance-taking Magistrate. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt; text-indent: 0in; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">12.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Now, let us see whether the cognizance-taking Magistrate dealt with the matter of taking cognizance accurately. On 24.07.2012, the learned Senior Judicial Magistrate (cognizance-taking Magistrate) in taking cognizance passed the following Order: </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 0.5in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Seen. In view of the Judicial Enquiry Report, cognizance u/s 302/201/34 of the Penal Code is taken against the sent-up in c/s accused. Not-sent-up in c/s accused are discharged. The case is ready for trial, hence the same is sent to learned Sessions Court for necessary step. Next date 28.08.12.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">13.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">A mere perusal of the above Order gives this Court an understanding that the learned Magistrate was entrusted with the duty of cognizance-taking Court before being sufficiently seasoned by gaining experiences in magisterial functions. The function of taking cognizance by a Judicial Magistrate is an important step in our criminal justice system and, in taking cognizance, while, in a CR case, a Judicial Magistrate is duty bound to examine and consider the complaint petition, statements, made by the complainant under Section 200 CrPC and if there is a Judicial Enquiry, then, the Judicial Enquiry Report and, likewise,<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>in the GR case, the FIR, the statements made by the witnesses under Sections 161 &amp; 164 CrPC, the statements made by the accused under Section 164 CrPC, the police report and the other incriminating materials, but s/he is not bound by the findings and recommendations of <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">the police report or the Judicial Enquiry Report,</span> for, the law envisages that the cognizance-taking Magistrate shall be completely independent in having the primary satisfaction as to whether there is any <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">prima facie</em> evidence of commission of offence by any accused for taking cognizance. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">14.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">In other words, when a criminal case is filed in the Police Station by lodging an FIR and, after investigation of the said case, the Investigating Officer (IO) submits a police report recommending for prosecuting or discharging any accused person, the Magistrate is not bound by the recommendation made by the IO for discharging any accused; rather the Magistrate is at liberty to take cognizance of any offence against any person either on the basis of the materials available before the Magistrate, such as, the police report or on the basis of his own knowledge/suspicion or information received from any person other than a police officer under Section 190 (1)(b) or under Section 190 (1)(c) of the CrPC, as the case may be. And, when a criminal case is filed through Court by presenting a petition of complaint/oral complaint before a Magistrate, the Magistrate is at liberty either to take cognizance directly under Section 190 (1)(a) of the CrPC on the basis of the statements made by the complainant under Section 200 CrPC or, <span style="letter-spacing: -.4pt;">in the event that the complaint is sent for Judicial</span><span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;"> Enquiry, on the basis of Judicial Enquiry</span> Report. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">15.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">However, in a GR case, after submission of the police report by the IO when a Naraji petition (complaint petition) is filed and the Magistrate directs for Judicial Enquiry, then, upon receiving the Judicial Enquiry Report, the Magistrate is at liberty to take cognizance either on the basis of the Judicial Enquiry Report, treating the same as <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">‘information received from any person other than a police officer’</em> under Section 190 (1)(c) of the CrPC or on the basis of his own knowledge/suspicion under Section 190 of the CrPC or on the basis of the police report under Section 190 (1)(b) of the CrPC or on the basis of complaint (Naraji petition) under Section 190 (1)(a) of the CrPC following finding <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">prima-facie</em> truthfulness as to the allegation made in the complaint petition through carrying out Judicial Enquiry. It is to be borne in mind by the learned cognizance-taking Magistrates of the land that the only source of the power of taking cognizance of any offence by the Magistrates of Bangladesh is Section 190 of the CrPC and, therefore, at the time of taking cognizance, it is a pivotal duty for a Magistrate to see whether taking cognizance of any offence fits into or relates to any of the clauses of sub-Section (1) of Section 190 of the CrPC. Thus, a Judicial Magistrate or <span style="letter-spacing: -.2pt;">an Executive Magistrate having been empowered</span> to take cognizance by the Government under Section 190(4) of the CrPC is competent to take cognizance of any offence against any person in addition to the person recommended by the IO and/or the Enquiring Magistrate resorting to any of the provisions which are enumerated under sub-Section (1) of Section 190 of the CrPC. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">16.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">In this case, the learned Magistrate took cognizance of the offence under Sections 302/201/34 of the Penal Code only against two persons and discharged others simply saying that <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">“in view of the Judicial Enquiry Report cognizance under Sections 302/201/34 of the Penal Code is taken against the sent-up accused in c/s”.</em> Evidently, the learned Magistrate did a stereotyped job, for, there is no effort on his part to assess the statements of the JWs by himself independently and thereby form his own view as to whether any statements have been made by the JWs implicating the persons who have been named in the petition of complaint. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">17.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">The proposition of law, as emanates from our research, that after receiving the Judicial Enquiry Report, when the Magistrate is going to pass an order on taking cognizance of any offence against any person/s under Section 190 (1) of the CrPC or dismissal of the complaint under Section 203 of the CrPC, it is incumbent upon the Magistrate to examine and consider the nitty-gritty of the Judicial Enquiry Report. In other words, to meticulously look at the statements of JWs and the comments on the <span style="letter-spacing: -.4pt;">demeanour of the JWs, findings and observations</span> on other steps taken by the Enquiring Magistrate and, then, upon considering the above materials, if the Magistrate is <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">prime-facie</em> satisfied that the report prepared by the Enquiring Magistrate, recommending for prosecuting all the accused named in the complaint petition or opining to prosecute a few and discharge others or proposing for discharge of all the accused, is entirely acceptable, s/he may either accept the same in toto or may take cognizance against the not-sent-up accused together with the sent-up accused upon recording her/his own opinion on the Judicial Enquiry Report so as to be evident on record that the Magistrate did not seek to stereotypically agree or disagree with the <span style="letter-spacing: -.2pt;">comments/remarks /findings/observations made</span> or the views expressed by the Enquiring Magistrate and, thereby, the cognizance-taking Magistrate has independently formed her/his own opinion in taking cognizance of the offence or dismissing the petition of complaint. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">18.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Applying the above ‘<em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">ratio’</em> of the law laid down by us hereinbefore in examining the legality and propriety of the Judicial Enquiry Report, it appears to us that the cognizance-taking Magistrate failed to deal with the issue of taking cognizance as per the provisions of law and, accordingly, the Order passed by the Magistrate dated 24.07.2012 in taking cognizance of the offence only against two persons upon dropping off the names of four accused is liable to be quashed. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">19.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">          </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">With our above findings and decision on the impugned Order dated 24.07.2012 passed by the learned Senior Judicial Magistrate, Gaibandha (cognizance-taking Magistrate), the other impugned Order dated 09.01.2014 passed by the learned Senior Sessions Judge, Gaibandha in Criminal Revision No. 271 of 2012 is also destined to be quashed. It is pertinent to record here that when this Court had asked the learned Advocate for the informant-petitioner for not taking any step for disposal of the Rule, in reply thereto, it was contended that the petitioner was reluctant to proceed with the enquiry and trial of this case during the period the learned Senior Sessions Judge is stationed at Gaibandha Judgeship, for, he was simply counting his time to go on <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">retirement and was not conscientiously performing</span> his duties. Some other derogatory comments about the quality of his performance, which had been performed just before his retirement, were also heard from the Bar, but those are ignored to register here. It transpires from the annexed papers that the learned Sessions Judge took two years to dispose of this revisional application against an Order of a Magistrate, albeit the issue of adjudication was very petty in nature.
